Ruling on Game That Disappears After Being Shot: Continuous Search Required
Ruling on game that endures arrow shot and disappears before found dead

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game found dead after disappearance and identify the condition of continuous search for permissibility.

Core Idea
A game found dead after shooting is only halal if the hunter searched continuously; stopping makes it haram.

This passage addresses a common hunting scenario: after shooting an arrow, the animal flees and disappears. When found dead later, the ruling depends on whether the hunter actively tracked it. The Hanafi school, based on authentic hadith, requires continuous search to attribute the death to the arrow and thus permit consumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is hunting with an arrow; the game endures and disappears. The ruling is conditional: if searched continuously until found dead → halal; if stopped searching → haram.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is from hadith of Adi ibn Hatim and Abu Tha'laba. The Prophet allowed eating game found after one or two days with only the arrow's mark, and also allowed eating if the hunter tracks for days.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit permission 'fakul' indicates halal when the arrow is the sole visible cause. The condition of continuous search is derived from the hadith where the Prophet permitted after tracking, implying that abandonment negates certainty.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may permit unconditionally if the arrow is found, but Hanafi requires active pursuit to eliminate doubt about alternative causes.
5
Determine the practical application
Apply the condition: if hunter searched continuously, it is halal; if he stopped, it is haram. Also consider the prohibition if the game falls into water or becomes putrid.

Source Text

[2588] (9) If the game is hit by an arrow, it endures and disappears from the hunter, and he continues searching until he finds it dead, then it is eaten. But if he stops searching and then finds it dead, it is not eaten. In a hadith: from Adi ibn Hatim from the Prophet (s) who said: '... And if you shoot at game and find it after a day or two with no mark but your arrow, then eat. But if it falls into water, do not eat.' (Bukhari, Muslim). From this hadith it is understood that it died from your arrow; if it is certain that it did not die from water, then even if found dead after two days, you may eat, provided it has not become putrid. Because in another hadith: from Abu Tha'laba from the Prophet (s): 'If you shoot your arrow and it disappears from you, then you find it, eat it as long as it has not become putrid.' (Muslim). And if he continues searching and finds it dead, he eats; if he stops and then finds it dead, he does not eat. The proof is this hadith: from Adi that he said to the Prophet (s): 'I shoot game and then trace its tracks for two or three days, then find it dead with my arrow in it.' He said: 'Eat if you wish.' (Bukhari, Abu Dawud, etc.). From this hadith it is understood that if he continues searching, it is permissible; if he stops, it is not permissible, because it is possible that it did not die from his arrow but from some other cause.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a specific hunting scenario: when a hunter shoots an arrow at game, the game endures the hit and disappears from sight. The key factor determining permissibility is the hunter's continuity of search. If the hunter continues searching until he finds the animal dead, it is halal to eat, based on the established principle (Zahir al-Riwayah) that the death is attributed to the arrow. The hadith of Adi ibn Hatim explicitly allows eating game found after one or two days as long as only the arrow's effect is present and the meat has not spoiled. However, if the hunter stops searching and later finds the animal dead, it is not halal because the means of death becomes uncertain; it might have died from another cause. The hadith from Adi about searching for two or three days supports this: if he continues the quest, he may eat; if he abandons it, he may not. This is a standard Hanafi ruling: the permissibility depends on the hunter's active pursuit and the certainty that the arrow caused death.

Key Terms
تحامل (tahammul): The game enduring the arrow wound and fleeing; it implies the animal's ability to move away after being hit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is halal because the hunter searched continuously.
B) It is haram because the deer disappeared.
C) It is halal only if found within one day.
D) It is haram because the deer might have died from thirst.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, game found dead after being shot is permissible only if the hunter maintained continuous search to attribute death to the arrow.

Hunting Game that Falls into Water: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling with explicit hadith exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game that falls into water after being shot, identify the hadith proof, and explain the underlying principle of uncertainty.

Core Idea
If the game falls into water before recovery, uncertainty in cause of death makes it haram.

Hunting is a permissible means of acquiring food, but it comes with specific conditions. One critical scenario arises when the animal falls into water after being shot. This lesson explores the clear Hanafi ruling on this matter, supported by an explicit prophetic hadith. Understanding this principle prevents unintentional consumption of haram meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the specific scenario? The shooter's arrow strikes the game, and it falls into water. The ruling: it is not eaten (haram).
This is a sub-case of hunting rules.
2
Locate the hadith proof
The hadith from Adi ibn Hatim (ra) is cited directly. It states: 'except if you find it has fallen into water, for you do not know whether the water killed it or your arrow.'
The hadith is found in Muslim, Bukhari, and Abu Dawud.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The exception (istithna) is explicit: 'illa an tajidahu qad waqa'a fi ma'. The reasoning ('illa) given is lack of knowledge about the cause of death. This makes the prohibition definitive (qati' al-dalala) despite the hadith being ahad in transmission.
The uncertainty (shubha) is the operative cause.
4
Note the Hanafi principle
Certainty is required for permissibility. Any doubt about the cause of death renders the animal haram. This principle extends to similar situations where the animal might have died from something other than the hunter's deliberate act.
This is a consistent Hanafi rule in hunting and slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۸۹] (۱۰) اگر شکار کو تیر مارا اور وہ پانی میں گر گیا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا۔
کیونکہ بہت ممکن ہے کہ تیر سے نہ مرا ہو بلکہ پانی سے شکار مرا ہو اس لئے حلال نہیں ہے (۲) حدیث میں کئی مرتبہ گزرا۔ عــن عــدی بــن حاتم قال سألت رسول الله الا الله عن الصيد قال اذا رميت بسهمک فاذكر اسم الله فان وجدته قد قتل فكل الا ان تجده قد وقع في ماء فانك لا تدرى الماء قتله او سهمک (ب) (مسلم شریف، باب الصيد بالكلاب المعلمة والرمی ص ۱۴۵ نمبر ۴۲۹۸۲/۱۹۲۹ / بخاری شریف، باب الصید اذا غاب عنه یو مین او ثلاثی ص ۸۲۴ نمبر ۵۴۸۴) را بو داؤد شریف ، باب فی الصید ص ۳۷ نمبر ۲۸۵۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ شکار پانی میں گر گیا ہو اور وہاں سے مردہ نکالا تو نہ کھائے کیونکہ کیا معلوم کہ پانی سے ہی مرا ہو تیر سے نہیں۔
[2589] (10) If game is shot with an arrow and it falls into water, it is not eaten. Because it is very possible that it did not die from the arrow but from the water, therefore it is not halal. The hadith has passed several times: from Adi ibn Hatim: 'I asked the Messenger of Allah about game. He said: If you shoot your arrow and mention Allah's name, then if you find it killed, eat it, except if you find it has fallen into water, for you do not know whether the water killed it or your arrow.' (Muslim, Bukhari, Abu Dawud). From this hadith it is understood that if the game has fallen into water and it is taken out dead, do not eat, because one does not know whether it died from the water or from the arrow.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning a specific problematic scenario in hunting: when the shot animal falls into water before being recovered. The ruling is clear: it is not permissible to eat such game. The basis for this is the uncertainty regarding the cause of death - it could be drowning rather than the arrow wound. The hadith from Adi ibn Hatim, narrated in multiple authentic collections, explicitly excludes this case from permissibility: 'except if you find it has fallen into water, for you do not know whether the water killed it or your arrow.' This is a well-established principle in the Hanafi school: any doubt about the cause of death renders the animal haram. The same principle applies to any situation where the animal might have died from something other than the hunter's deliberate act. This ruling is part of the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
شُبْهَة (shubha): Uncertainty or doubt regarding the lawful status or cause of death.
صَيْد (syd): Game, the animal being hunted.
اِسْتِثْنَاء (istithna): Exception, as in 'except if you find it has fallen into water'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots a bird with an arrow. The bird falls into a river and is found dead. What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Halal, because the hunter intended to hunt and recited tasmiyah.
B) Haram, because the cause of death is uncertain between the arrow and drowning.
C) Halal, because the arrow wound is visible and the bird died quickly.
D) Permissible only if the hunter retrieves the bird within an hour.

Key Takeaway
Game that falls into water after being shot is not halal due to uncertainty in cause of death, as established by the explicit hadith exception.

Game Falling from Height After Being Shot
Ruling on game that falls from roof or mountain after arrow shot

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game that falls from a height after being shot, explain the Quranic proof (al-mutaraddiyah) and the athar of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, and distinguish the Hanafi position from other schools.

Core Idea
Uncertainty about whether the fall or arrow caused death renders game haram, based on Quran and Companion practice.

This lesson covers a specific hunting scenario: after being shot, game falls from a roof or mountain and dies. The Hanafi school prohibits consumption due to doubt about the cause of death. The evidence includes the Quranic term 'al-mutaraddiyah' and an athar from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the principle that halal slaughter requires certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is hunting game with an arrow, but the game falls and rolls from a height before dying. The ruling is haram (not halal).
The ruling applies even if the arrow was the initial cause.
2
Locate the dalil
Two proofs: the Quranic verse (5:3) prohibiting al-mutaraddiyah (that which falls from a height) and an athar from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud forbidding eating game that falls from a mountain.
The Quranic proof is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalala; the athar provides a concrete application.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'al-mutaraddiyah' in the verse covers death by fall. The doubt (shubha) that the fall may have caused death triggers the prohibition. The athar reinforces this by explicitly stating the fear that the fall killed it.
The principle: certainty of lawful slaughter is required; doubt prevents halal.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Maliki, Shafi'i) may allow if the arrow wound is clearly fatal and the fall is incidental. They rely on general permissibility of hunting.
Hanafi response: the specific prohibition and the doubt take precedence over general permissibility.
5
Apply the principle
In practice, Hanafis avoid game that has fallen from a height after being shot, unless it is certain the arrow caused death before the fall (e.g., the animal dies on the roof without rolling).
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۹۰] (۱۱) ایسے ہی اگر چھت پر یا پہاڑ پر گر ا پھر وہاں سے زمیں تک لڑھکا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا۔
شکار کو مارا جس کی وجہ سے شکار چھت پر یا پہاڑ پر گرا اور وہاں سے لڑھکتے ہوئے زمین پر گرا تو وہ شکار حلال نہیں ہے۔ یہ معلوم نہیں ہے کہ تیر سے مرا ہے یا پہاڑ یا چھت سے لڑھکنے کی وجہ سے مرا ہے۔ اگر لڑ سکنے کی وجہ سے مرا ہو تو حلال نہیں ہے۔ آیت میں ہے۔والمنخنقة والموقوذة والمتردية ( آيت ٣ سورة المائدة (۵) اور چونکہ یہاں تیرا اور لڑھکنے میں شبہ پیدا ہو گیا اس لئے حلال نہیں ہے (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ قال عبد الله اذا رمى احدكم صيدا فتردى من جبل فمات فلا تأكلوا فاني اخاف ان يكون التردى قتله (ج) (سن للمبتى ، باب الصيد يرمي فيقع على جبل ثمر تر دی منہ او يقع في الماءوج تاسع ص ۴۱۶ نمبر ۱۸۹۴۲ار مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ۱۷ اذاری صیدا فوقع فی الماء ج رابع ص ۲۴۸ نمبر ۱۹۶۸۴ / مصنف عبدالرزاق نمبر ۸۴۶۲) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ پہاڑ سے لڑھکا ہو تو حلال نہیں ہے۔
[2590] (11) Similarly, if it falls on a roof or a mountain, then rolls from there to the ground, it is not eaten. The game is shot, causing it to fall on a roof or mountain, and then it rolls to the ground; that game is not halal. It is not known whether it died from the arrow or from rolling down the mountain or roof. If it died from rolling, it is not halal. The verse says: 'and that which has been strangled, and that which has been beaten, and that which has fallen from a height...' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:3). And because here there is doubt between the arrow and the rolling, it is not halal. There is an athar (report from a Companion): 'Abdullah [ibn Mas'ud?] said: If any of you shoots game and it falls from a mountain and dies, do not eat it, for I fear that the fall killed it.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq). From this athar it is understood that if it rolled from a mountain, it is not halal.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is the third ruling in this series, dealing with a scenario where the arrow hits the game, causing it to fall onto a roof or mountain ledge, and then it rolls or falls down to the ground. The ruling is that such game is not halal. The reasoning is analogous to the water case: there is uncertainty about the cause of death. The death may have been caused by the fall rather than the arrow. The author cites the Quranic verse from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:3) which prohibits 'al-mutaraddiyah' (that which falls from a height), indicating that death from a fall renders the animal carrion and unlawful. Even though the arrow was the initial cause, the fall introduces a new potential cause, creating shubha (doubt). The Hanafi school, following the principle that certainty of lawful slaughter is required, rules it impermissible. An athar (report) from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) supports this: he explicitly forbids eating game that falls from a mountain because he fears the fall killed it. This shows the practice of the early Muslims. The ruling is part of the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
المتردية (al-mutaraddiyah): An animal that falls from a height and dies as a result; one of the categories of prohibited carrion in the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Halal, because the arrow hit it first and the fall was inevitable.
B) Halal, because the hunter recited tasmiyah.
C) Haram, because the death may have been from the fall.
D) Haram, but only if the goat was already dead before hitting the ground.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits eating game that falls from a height after being shot because the fall may have caused death, making it analogous to the Quranic al-mutaraddiyah.

Hunting: Game Falling to Ground Initially
States ruling on game that falls to ground after being hit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game that falls to the ground after being hit and explain why doubt about the cause of death is disregarded.

Core Idea
If hunted game falls to the ground, it is lawful; doubt over cause of death is ignored.

In hunting, a common scenario is that an animal falls to the ground after being hit. The question arises: is it permissible to eat if there is uncertainty whether the arrow killed it or the fall? The Hanafi school addresses this by giving benefit of the doubt to the hunter, as long as the fall is the natural consequence of the shot. This lesson explains the ruling and its underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is hunting game that falls to ground after being hit. The ruling is that it is eaten (halal).
The ruling is unconditional regarding the cause of death.
2
Locate the underlying principle
The principle is that doubt about the cause of death is disregarded because the game inevitably falls to ground after being shot.
This is based on istihsan or the continuity of the original permissibility.
3
Apply to a similar scenario
The text extends the ruling to game that initially falls to ground and then later reaches a mountain or roof; it is still eaten.
The initial fall to ground is the key factor.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools require certainty that the arrow caused death. The Hanafi school does not require this certainty when the fall is inevitable.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (difference of opinion) in fiqh.
5
Evaluate the practical effect
For the Hanafi hunter, this ruling provides flexibility: they can eat the game without needing to verify the cause of death.
This eases the practice of hunting for food.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۲۵۹۱ (۱۲) وان وقع على الارض ابتداء اكل [۲۵۹۱] (۱۲) اور اگر ابتدا میں زمین پر گرا تو کھایا جائے گا۔ ا شکار تیر کھا کر زمین پر گرا اور مرگیا پھر کسی طرح ہو اوغیرہ کے ذریعہ پہاڑ پر یا چھت پر پہنچ گیا تو کھایا جائے گا۔ اس میں بھی اگر چہ شک ہے کہ تیر سے نہ مرا ہو بلکہ زمین پر گرنے کی وجہ سے مرا ہو۔ لیکن چونکہ شکار آخر زمین پر ہی گرے گا اس لئے یہاں اس شک کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جائے گا اور شکار حلال رہے گا۔
And if it falls to the ground initially, it is eaten. And if initially it falls to the ground, then it is eaten. That game which is hit by an arrow, falls to the ground and dies, then later through any means reaches a mountain or roof, it is eaten. In this also, although there is doubt that it may not have died from the arrow but from falling to the ground, however since the game ultimately falls to the ground, this doubt is not considered and the game remains lawful.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on hunting. The ruling is a statement: if a hunter shoots an animal and it initially falls to the ground (rather than being caught in mid-air or on a mountain), it is permissible to eat even if there is uncertainty whether the animal died from the arrow or from the fall. The commentator explains that since the game naturally falls to the ground, the doubt is disregarded (istihsan or principle of continuity). The ruling is based on the general permissibility of hunting with arrows, assuming proper intent and conditions. No specific proof is cited here, but the principle is consistent with the Hanafi approach of giving benefit of the doubt to the hunter.

Key Terms
صید (sayd): Hunting, especially game that is lawful to hunt.
شک (shakk): Doubt or uncertainty about the cause of death.
ابتداء (ibtidaa): Initially, at first instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a hunter shoots a bird and it initially falls to the ground, what is the ruling?
A) It is not eaten because the fall might have killed it.
B) It is eaten because the doubt about the cause of death is disregarded.
C) It is only eaten if the hunter sees the arrow hit a vital organ.
D) It is makruh to eat it.

Key Takeaway
If hunted game initially falls to the ground, it is lawful to eat even if there is doubt whether the arrow or the fall caused death.

Hunting with Miirad: Broadside vs. Sharp Edge
Ruling with proof on hunting with miirad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for game killed by the broadside versus the sharp edge of a miirad, and explain the scriptural evidence from Qur'an and hadith.

Core Idea
A miirad that kills by broadside makes the animal mawqudhah (haram); only a wounding blow constitutes dhabh idtirari (halal).

In classical hunting practice, the miirad—a javelin-like arrow—could strike game in two ways: with its sharp edge or its flat broadside. The Hanafi school draws a critical distinction between these two outcomes, grounded in the Qur'anic prohibition of mawqudhah (animals killed by a violent blow) and the Prophet's instruction to Adi ibn Hatim. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its basis in primary texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The miirad can hit game with its sharp edge (hadd) or its broadside (ard). Determine which part struck the animal.
The ruling depends on whether the implement cuts/wounds or merely crushes/bludgeons.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
If the sharp edge wounds and causes blood flow, the meat is halal (dhabh idtirari). If the broadside kills by blunt force, the meat is haram (mawqudhah).
3
Locate the primary dalil
The Qur'an (5:3) lists al-mawqudhah (killed by a violent blow) as forbidden. The hadith of Adi ibn Hatim explicitly states: 'If you hit with its sharp edge, eat; if you hit with its broadside and it kills, then it is wuqidh, so do not eat.'
Both sources are qat'i al-thubut (certain transmission) and qat'i al-dalala (clear meaning).
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The broadside kill falls under mawqudhah because the animal dies from the force of the blow without a wound. The sharp edge creates a wound that allows blood to flow, effecting dhabh idtirari.
The key distinction is wounding vs. blunt force.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider the broadside kill permissible if the hunter intended to hunt. Hanafis strictly prohibit it due to the clear textual evidence.
The practical consequence: Hanafis do not eat such game; others may.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۹۲ (۱۳) وما اصاب المعراض بعرضه لم يوكل وان جرحه اكل [۲۵۹۲] (۱۳) کسی شکار کو لگے۔ بھال کا تیر چوڑائی کی جانب سے تو نہ کھایا جائے گا۔ تیر کا دھار دار حصہ شکار کو نہیں لگا بلکہ لکڑی کا حصہ لگا اور شکار گویا کہ لکڑی کے دباؤ سے مر گیا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا۔ شکار زخمی نہیں ہوا اس لئے ذبح اضطراری نہیں ہوا۔ وہ آیت کے مطابق موقوذہ ہو گیا یعنی دبوچ کر مارا گیا ہوا اس لئے یہ حلال نہیں ہے۔ آیت یہ ہے۔ حرمت عليكم الميتة والدم ولحم الخنزير وما اهل لغير الله به والمنخنقة والموقوذة والمترديه و النطيحة وما اكل السبع الا ما ذكيتم وما ذبح على النصب (الف) (آیت ۳ سورۃ المائدۃ ۵) اس آیت میں موقوفہ یعنی دباؤ سے مرے ہوئے کو حرام قرار دیا گیا ہے (۲) حدیث میں کئی مرتبہ صراحت گزری۔ عن عدی بن حاتم ... وسألته عن صيد المعراض فقال اذا اصبت بحده فكل واذا اصبت بعرضه فقتل فانه وقيذ فلا تأكل (ب) ( بخاری شریف ، باب از او جد مع الصيد كلبا آخرص ۸۲۴ نمبر ۱۵۳۸۶ مسلم شریف، باب الصيد بالكلاب المعلمة والرمی ص ۱۴۵ نمبر ۴۹۷۴/۱۹۲۹) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ تیر کی چوڑائی کا حصہ لگا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا کیونکہ وہ وقیذ ہو گیا یعنی دباؤ سے مرا ہوا ہو گیا۔ اور دھار دار حصہ لگے تو کھایا جائے گا کیونکہ وہ ذبح اضطراری ہو گیا۔ اور اوپر حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔اذا اصبت بحدہ فكل۔
And what the miirad hits with its broadside is not eaten, and if it wounds it, it is eaten. If it hits the game with the broadside of the spear-like arrow, it is not eaten. The sharp part of the arrow did not hit the game, but the wooden part hit, and the game died from the pressure of the wood, so it is not eaten. The game was not wounded, so it did not become dhabh idtirari (emergency slaughter). According to the verse, it became mawqudhah, i.e., killed by beating, so it is not halal. The verse is: 'Forbidden to you are dead animals, blood, the flesh of swine, and that which has been dedicated to other than Allah, and those killed by strangling, or by a violent blow, or by a fall, or by goring, and those eaten by a wild animal, except what you slaughter, and those sacrificed on stone altars.' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:3). In this verse, mawqudhah, i.e., killed by pressure, is declared forbidden. And the hadith has been mentioned many times: from Adi ibn Hatim... he asked about hunting with the miirad, and the Prophet said: 'If you hit with its sharp edge, eat; if you hit with its broadside and it kills, then it is wuqidh (killed by pressure), so do not eat.' (Bukhari, Muslim). From this hadith, it is known that if the broadside hits, it is not eaten because it becomes wuqidh, i.e., killed by pressure; and if the sharp edge hits, it is eaten because it becomes dhabh idtirari. And in the above hadith there is evidence for this: 'If you hit with its sharp edge, eat.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding hunting with a miirad (a type of arrow or spear without a sharp point on the sides). The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: when the animal is struck by the broadside (the flat part) and when struck by the sharp edge. If the broadside kills, the animal is considered mawqudhah (killed by a blow), which is explicitly forbidden in the Qur'an (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:3). The commentator explains that since the animal was not wounded (no blood flow), it does not qualify as dhabh idtirari (emergency slaughter). Instead, it falls under the category of mawqudhah. The Prophet's hadith (from Adi ibn Hatim) provides the same ruling: if the sharp edge hits, eat; if the broadside kills, do not eat. The hadith uses the term 'wuqidh' for the broadside kill. Thus, the Hanafi position is clear: for the meat to be halal, the hunting implement must cut or wound the animal, causing blood flow, thereby effecting a form of slaughter. Striking with a blunt force does not constitute proper slaughter.

Key Terms
معراض (miirad): A type of hunting arrow or javelin that has a sharp edge but also a broad side; used in pre-Islamic and early Islamic hunting.
موقوذة (mawqudhah): An animal killed by a violent blow without cutting or wounding.
ذبح اضطراري (dhabh idtirari): Emergency slaughter caused by a hunting implement that wounds the animal, making it permissible without the regular slaughtering process.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter uses a miirad and the animal dies from being hit by the broadside (flat part), what is the ruling?
A) Halal because the hunter intended to hunt.
B) Haram because it is mawqudhah.
C) Makruh but permissible if needed.
D) Halal only if the animal was not killed instantly.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of game meat hinges on whether the implement wounds (dhabh idtirari) or kills by blunt force (mawqudhah).

Hunting with Pellet (Bunduqah): Not Eaten
States ruling and proof for game killed by pellet from a slingshot.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a round clay pellet and explain why it is classified as mawqudhah (forbidden by blow).

Core Idea
A game killed by a round clay pellet is haram because it is mawqudhah — killed by blunt force, not wounding.

This lesson addresses a specific hunting implement: the bunduqah, a round clay pellet used in a slingshot. The Hanafi school rules that game killed by such a pellet is not permissible to eat. We will examine the reasoning and the proof from the Qur'an and the practice of the Companion Ibn Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implement and its effect
The bunduqah is a round clay pellet shot from a slingshot. It kills by crushing the flesh and bone, not by cutting or wounding.
2
Recognize the classification as mawqudhah
Mawqudhah (killed by a violent blow) is explicitly forbidden in the Qur'an (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:3). The bunduqah's effect fits this category.
The Arabic term 'موقوذة' is key.
3
Cite the evidence
The passage cites an athar from Ibn Umar: 'the animal killed by the bunduqah is mawqudhah.' This confirms the application.
Hadith reference: Bukhari, Bab Sayd.
4
Apply the ruling
Because the pellet does not wound, the animal does not undergo dhabh idtirari (emergency slaughter through wounding). Hence, the meat is haram.
The condition for halal hunting is that the implement must cut or wound.
5
Note the modern adaptation
Later Hanafi scholars differentiate modern pointed bullets (which wound) from round pellets; the former are permissible.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on change in the implement's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۹۳] (۱۴) ولايوكل ما اصابه البندقة اذا مات [۲۵۹۳] (۱۴) نہیں کھایا جائے گا جس کو غلیل کی گولی لگے اگر وہ اس سے مرجائے۔ پچھلے زمانے میں گول گول مٹی کی گولی بناتے تھے اور غلیل پر رکھ کر شکار کرتے تھے وہ چونکہ مٹی کا ہوتا تھا اور گول ہوتا تھا اس لئے وہ شکار کو زخمی نہیں کرتا تھا بلکہ اس کے لگنے سے موقوف کی شکل بنتی یعنی گوشت ہڈی چور کر دیتا اور شکار مر جاتا ۔ چونکہ اس صورت میں ذبح اضطراری کی شکل نہیں بنی اس لئے شکار حلال نہیں ہے۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن عـمـرانـه كـان يقول في المقتولة بالبندقية تلك الموقوذة ((ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب صید منها ۲۵۹۴) (۱۵) واذا رمى صيدا فقطع عضوا منه أكل الصيد ولم يوكل العضو
And what the pellet hits if it dies is not eaten. That which is hit by a slingshot pellet, if it dies from it, is not eaten. In earlier times, they used to make round clay pellets and place them on a slingshot to hunt. Since it was made of clay and round, it did not wound the animal but rather when it hit, it would crush the flesh and bone, and the animal would die. Since in this case, the form of dhabh idtirari did not occur, the game is not halal. There is an athar: from Ibn Umar, he used to say: 'The one killed by the bunduqah is mawqudhah.' (Bukhari, Bab Sayd...).
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof regarding hunting with a bunduqah (a round clay pellet shot from a slingshot). The ruling states that if the game dies from being hit by such a pellet, it is not eaten. The commentary explains that the pellet is round and made of clay, so it does not cut or wound the animal; it crushes and kills by blunt force. This makes the animal mawqudhah (killed by a violent blow), which is forbidden by the Qur'an. The commentator cites an athar (statement) from Ibn Umar, who explicitly said that an animal killed by a bunduqah is mawqudhah. This confirms that such meat is haram. The Hanafi school thus requires that for hunting to be permissible, the implement must wound the animal (cause blood flow) to effect dhabh idtirari. The bunduqah does not satisfy this condition.

Key Terms
بندقة (bunduqah): A round clay pellet used with a slingshot for hunting; kills by crushing blunt force.
موقوذة (mawqudhah): Killed by a violent blow without cutting; forbidden in Qur'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Halal because it is hunted.
B) Haram because it is mawqudhah.
C) Halal only if the hunter recites Bismillah.
D) Makruh but permissible in necessity.

Key Takeaway
Game killed by a round pellet that crushes without wounding is haram (mawqudhah); the implement must cause bleeding for halal hunting.

Hunting: Limb Cut Off, Animal Eaten, Limb Not
Ruling and proof for when a limb is severed by hunting implement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on severed limbs from game animals and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A limb cut from a living animal is carrion; the body is halal.

When hunting with a tool that severs a limb before the animal dies, a specific fiqh distinction applies. The body of the game is permissible, but the severed limb is not. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its evidence from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
What is the legal status of the game's body and the severed limb? State both clearly.
Body = halal, limb = haram.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the hadith cited and its source. Who narrated it?
Hadith of Abu Waqid al-Laythi in Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud.
3
Analyse the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the hadith's wording prove the ruling? Identify the key phrase.
'Ma quti'a min al-bahimah wa hiya hayyah' covers any limb cut while alive.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Which schools might disagree and on what basis?
Some may consider the limb halal if cut by the same implement that killed.
5
Confirm the Mufta Bihi
What is the operative Hanafi position for fatwa?
As stated: body halal, limb haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۹۴] (۱۵) واذا رمى صيدا فقطع عضوا منه أكل الصيد ولم يوكل العضو [۲۵۹۴] (۱۵) اگر شکار پر تیر مارا اور اس سے کوئی عضو کاٹ دیا تو شکار کھایا جائے گا اور عضو نہیں کھایا جائے گا۔ شکار پر اس طرح تیر مارا کہ مثلا اس کا پاؤں کٹ کر جدا ہو گیا اور پورا جانور الگ ہو گیا تو جانور حلال ہے اور عضو مثلا پاؤں اب حلال نہیں ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابی واقد الليثى قال قدم النبي لالالالالاله المدينة وهم يجبون اسنمة الابل ويقطعون اليات الغنم فقال ماقطع من البهيمة وهي حية فهو ميتة (ب) ( ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء ماقطع من الحی فھومیت ص ۲۷۳ نمبر ۰ ۱۳۸ر ابوداؤد شریف، باب اذا قطع من الصيد قطعة ج ثانی ص ۳۸ نمبر ۲۸۵۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ زندہ جانور کا کوئی عضو کاٹ لے تو وہ عضو حلال نہیں ہے۔ یہی حال ہے کہ اگر ایسا تیر مارا کہ پاؤں کٹ کر الگ ہو گیا اور شکار مر گیا تو پاؤں حلال نہیں البتہ باقی شکار حلال ہے۔
And if he shoots a game and cuts off a limb from it, the game is eaten but the limb is not eaten. If he shoots a game with an arrow and cuts off a limb, the game is eaten but the limb is not eaten. For example, an arrow cuts off its foot separately and the whole animal is separated, then the animal is halal but the limb, e.g., the foot, is not halal. In a hadith: from Abu Waqid al-Laythi, he said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) came to Medina and they used to cut off the humps of camels and cut off the tails of sheep, so he said: 'Whatever is cut from an animal while it is alive, it is dead meat (murdar).' (Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud). From this hadith, it is known that if a limb is cut from a living animal, that limb is not halal. This is the case: if an arrow cuts off the foot separately and the game dies, the foot is not halal, but the rest of the game is halal.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof. The ruling: if a hunter shoots an animal and the arrow severs a limb (e.g., a foot) while the animal is still alive, the main body of the game is halal (because it died as a result of hunting), but the severed limb is not halal. The proof is a hadith from Abu Waqid al-Laythi: the Prophet prohibited eating parts cut from a living animal, declaring such parts 'murdar' (dead meat). This is a general principle: any part separated from a living animal is considered carrion and thus forbidden. The Hanafi school applies this specifically to hunting: if the implement severs a limb before death, that limb is carrion; the rest of the animal is halal because it died by the hunting implement (dhabh idtirari). The commentator emphasizes that the limb itself is not halal, even though the rest is.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): Dead meat or carrion; specifically, an animal that died without proper slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter's arrow severs a deer's leg and the deer dies from the wound, what is the ruling on the severed leg?
A) It is halal because the deer was hunted.
B) It is haram because it was cut from a living animal.
C) It is halal if the hunter intended to eat it.
D) It is makruh but permissible.

Key Takeaway
A limb severed from a game animal while it is alive is considered carrion and is haram, regardless of the animal's subsequent death from the hunt.

Ruling on Game Cut into Uneven Pieces
Ruling on eating game cut into three pieces with unequal sizes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating game cut into three pieces of unequal size and apply it to determine which parts are halal based on the location of the majority.

Core Idea
When game is cut into uneven pieces, the location of the majority determines which parts are eaten.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in hunting: when a hunter's arrow cuts the game into three pieces of different sizes. The Hanafi school provides a precise ruling based on which part—the head or the tail—contains the larger portion. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish lawful from unlawful meat in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the game is cut into three pieces (or two pieces with one larger) by a hunting arrow, and the pieces are of unequal size.
The text assumes the cut is made by a single arrow during the hunt.
2
Determine the location of the majority
Determine whether the larger piece (majority) is attached to the tail (hindquarters) or the head (front). The ruling depends on this.
The 'majority' refers to the larger portion by weight or size.
3
Apply the ruling
If the majority is towards the tail, all pieces are eaten. If the majority is towards the head, only the majority (the larger piece) is eaten.
The head is considered the more significant part; if the majority is with the head, the smaller piece is treated as a severed limb.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The head is considered the dominant part of the animal. Thus, if the large piece contains the head, it is treated as the main body; the small piece is treated as a separate, possibly impermissible part.
This is a detailed application of general hunting rules regarding the permissibility of parts cut during the kill.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۹۵] (۱۶) واذا قطعه اثلاثا والاكثر مما يلى العجز أكل الجميع وان كان الاكثر مما يلى الراس كل الاكثر [۲۵۹۵] (۱۶) اگر شکار کو اثلاث کانٹا اور اکثر سرین سے متصل ہے تو سب کو کھایا جائے گا اور اکثر سر سے متصل ہے تو اکثر کھایا جائے گا۔ ا شکار کو تیر سے دو ٹکڑے کر دیا اس طرح کہ ایک حصہ کم ہے اور دوسرا حصہ زیادہ ہے۔ مثلا ایک طرف تہائی ہے اور دوسری طرف دو تہائی
And if he cuts it into three pieces and the majority is towards the tail, all is eaten; and if the majority is towards the head, the majority is eaten. If he cuts the game into three pieces, and the larger part is attached to the tail, then all is eaten; and if the larger part is attached to the head, the larger part is eaten. That is, the game is cut into two pieces by an arrow such that one part is small and the other large. For example, one side is one-third and the other side is two-thirds.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on a specific scenario: when a hunter's arrow cuts the game into three pieces (or two pieces, but the discussion assumes three pieces or two pieces with one being larger). The ruling depends on which part is larger: if the larger portion is towards the tail (the hindquarters), then the entire animal is eaten (all three pieces). If the larger portion is towards the head (the front), then only the larger portion is eaten. The commentator explains with an example: if one piece is one-third and the other is two-thirds, and the two-thirds is towards the tail, all is eaten; if the two-thirds is towards the head, then only the two-thirds is eaten. The reasoning is based on the principle that the head is considered the more significant part, so if the majority is with the head, the head portion is considered the main body, and the smaller piece may be treated as a severed limb (though not explicitly stated here). The text does not provide proof, but the ruling is a detailed application of the general rules of hunting.

Key Terms
عجز (ajuz): The tail end or hindquarters of the animal.
رأس (ra's): The head or front part of the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter's arrow cuts a gazelle into three pieces, and the majority (two-thirds) is towards the head, what is eaten?
A) All three pieces.
B) Only the larger portion (two-thirds).
C) Only the piece containing the head.
D) Nothing because it is cut into pieces.

Key Takeaway
The edibility of game cut by a hunting arrow depends on the location of the majority piece: if majority is towards the tail, all is eaten; if towards the head, only the majority is eaten.

Ruling on a Hunted Animal Cut into Parts: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explains Hanafi ruling on halal parts when animal is cut by weapon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for determining halal parts of a hunted animal cut in half, and explain the Shafi'i counter-argument and its practical difference.

Core Idea
Head-side part is always halal; rump-side is halal only if it is not less than half.

This passage from Sharh al-Thamiri addresses a common hunting scenario: an animal is struck by a weapon and cut into two parts. The Hanafi school, relying on athar from Thawri and Ali, distinguishes between the head-side and rump-side parts. The head-side is treated like slaughter (dhabh) and is always halal. The rump-side's halal status depends on its proportion relative to the head-side. Imam Shafi'i takes a stricter view. Understanding this distinction clarifies a key Hanafi principle in hunting law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify which parts of the cut animal are halal according to Abu Hanifa. Note the condition for the rump-side part.
The head-side part is always halal. The rump-side is halal only if it is not less than half the animal (i.e., the head-side is the majority).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State the Shafi'i ruling on cut parts of a live animal. Their general principle is that any limb cut from a living animal is carrion.
Imam Shafi'i holds that if an animal is cut into parts while alive, all cut parts are unlawful unless the animal dies instantly.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence does the Shafi'i school cite? The hadith: 'Whatever is cut from a living animal is carrion.'
This is a strong textual proof (dalil naqli).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school address this? They distinguish the head-side part as analogous to slaughter, and apply a proportion rule based on the athar.
The head-side part is treated as dhabh, not as a cut limb. The rump-side is only problematic if it is less than half – otherwise it is not considered a separate limb.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two schools' rulings in a real hunting scenario?
Under Hanafi, if the head-side part is the majority, both parts are halal. Under Shafi'i, both parts are halal only if the animal died instantly; otherwise both are haram.

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الرابع
۱۵
كتاب الصيد
النت العجز سرین۔ یلی : ملا ہوا ہو، ساتھ ہو۔
The Sharh al-Thamiri, Part 4, 14. Book of Hunting. He said: 'What is adjacent to the head is eaten as the majority.' It is a share. That is, the hunted animal's hand or foot is not cut off, but rather its body is split into two pieces. Now here the principle is that the head is attached to the body, and when the head is cut off, it becomes slaughtered (dhabih). And if the head is separated from the body, then the body is also halal and the head is also halal. Therefore, if two-thirds of the body is on the rump side and one-third on the head side, then the rump part is halal and the head part is halal. As if the head was slaughtered, and in such a manner that some part of the body was cut with the head, and when the head separates from the body, both body and head are halal. Similarly, here both the rump and head are halal. (2) There is proof in the athar. Al-Thawri informed us, saying: 'If the two thighs are cut off and separated, do not eat the thighs, but eat what contains the head. If what is with the thighs is less than half of the wild animal, do not eat it, but eat what is adjacent to the head. If the two halves are equal, eat both of them, and whatever exceeds from the head side is eaten.' This is the saying of Abu Hanifa. (a) (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Chapter on Hunting when Part is Cut, Vol. 4, p. 263, no. 847). This hadith contains full detail: if the rump side is more, then both the head and rump are eaten. And if the head side is more and the rump side is less, then the head part is halal and the rump part is not halal. (2) In another athar: From Ali, he said: 'Leave what is separated, and eat what remains. If he cut it into two equal parts, then let him eat.' (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 18, on the man who strikes a hunting animal and a limb separates from it, Vol. 4, p. 249, no. 19995; Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Chapter on Hunting when Part is Cut, Vol. 4, p. 463, no. 847). In this athar, 'jazalahu jazlan' means that if the hunting animal is cut into two equal halves, then both are halal. Therefore, if the rump side is more than half, then by priority that part is eaten. And the head part is eaten because it is like slaughter. And if more than half on the head side is cut away and less than half remains on the rump side, then it is considered that a limb was cut from a living animal. And in the hadith above, it was mentioned that if a limb is cut from a living animal, it is not halal. Thus, this rump part is not halal. In the above athar, the saying of Thawri passed: 'If what is with the thighs is less than half of the wild animal, do not eat it, and eat what is adjacent to the head.' (c) (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, no. 847). In this athar, it is mentioned that if the rump side is less and the head side is more, then the rump side is not halal and the head side is halal. Imam Shafi'i says that if a limb of the hunting animal is cut off in such a way that the animal was alive and its hand, foot, or rump was cut off, then it is not eaten based on the above hadith. But if the animal's hand or foot is cut off and at the same time the animal dies and falls to the ground, then both the animal and the cut-off limb are halal. Footnote: (a) Hadrat Thawri said: If both thighs are cut from the animal and separated, then the thighs will not be eaten. And the part containing the head will be eaten. So if with the thighs there is less than half of the wild animal, do not eat the thighs, and eat what is adjacent to the head. So if both halves are equal, then both are eaten, and if more is on the head side, then eat. This is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. (b) It is narrated from Hadrat Ali: 'Leave what is separated from the animal, and eat what remains. If he cut it into two equal pieces, then eat.' (c) So if with the thighs there is less than half of the wild animal, do not eat it, and eat what is adjacent to the head.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Thamiri presents the Hanafi ruling on a hunted animal that has been cut into two parts by the hunting weapon. The key principle is that the head-side part is always considered halal because cutting the head is akin to proper slaughter (dhabh). The rump-side part is halal only if it does not exceed half of the animal; if it is less than half, it is treated as a limb cut from a living animal and thus unlawful. The author provides two athar (traditions) from Thawri and Ali to support this ruling. Imam Shafi'i differs: he holds that if the limb is cut while the animal is alive, it is unlawful, regardless of proportion, unless the animal dies instantly. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa, uses the analogy of slaughter and the principle that the head-side majority renders the entire catch halal.

Key Terms
یلى الرأس (yali al-ra's): Adjacent to the head; the part of the animal that includes the head and the area attached to it.
جزله جزلا (jazalahu jazlan): Cut into two equal halves; used in the athar of Ali to indicate that when an animal is cut into two equal parts, both are halal.
عجز ('ajuz): Rump; the posterior part of the animal, opposite the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the Hanafi view, if a hunted animal is cut into two parts where the rump side is two-thirds and the head side is one-third, what is the ruling?
A) Only the head part is halal.
B) Both parts are halal.
C) Only the rump part is halal.
D) Neither part is halal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi hunting law, the head-side part is always halal; the rump-side is halal only if it is not less than half of the animal.

Hunt of Magian, Apostate, and Idolater is Forbidden
States prohibition with qiyas and textual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a Magian, apostate, or idolater and identify the proofs from Quran, hadith, and athar that prohibit it.

Core Idea
Game killed by those without valid belief is haram, even if they recite Bismillah.

The Hanafi school extends the prohibition of meat from invalid slaughter to hunting. This lesson examines the ruling that game killed by a Magian, apostate, or idolater is forbidden, and the evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The game (hunted animal) that is killed by a Magian, apostate, or idolater. The ruling is that it is haram (not halal).
The ruling applies whether the hunting was done with a dog or arrow.
2
Locate the primary dalil (Quran)
The author cites Quran 6:121: 'Do not eat of that over which Allah's name has not been mentioned.' This is taken as proof that Bismillah is required for halal meat.
3
Examine the secondary dalil (hadith and athar)
A hadith requiring Bismillah at the time of hunting, and an athar from Jabir forbidding the slaughter and hunt of the Magian, are used to reinforce the ruling.
4
Apply the reasoning (qiyas) to other categories
By analogy, the same ruling applies to apostates and idolaters, as they also lack valid belief. Their Bismillah is invalid because they do not believe in Allah.
The Hanafi position is that even if they recite Bismillah, it is not accepted due to their lack of iman.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and thamarah
Some Shafi'i scholars may allow the hunt if the Magian recites Bismillah. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis forbid it absolutely, while others may permit it under that condition.

مجوسی نے یا مرتد نے بابت پرست نے اپنے کتے یا تیر سے شکار کیا ہو اور مسلمان کے ذبح کرنے سے پہلے مر گیا ہو تو وہ شکار حلال نہیں
(۱) یہ لوگ مسلمان بھی نہیں ہیں اور اہل کتاب بھی نہیں ہیں اس لئے ان لوگوں کا جس طرح ذبیحہ حلال نہیں ہے شکار بھی حلال نہیں ہے (۲) حدیث میں ہے کہ شکار کرتے وقت اور آیت میں ہے کہ ذبح کرتے وقت بسم اللہ پڑھے تب حلال ہوگا ۔ اور یہ لوگ بسم اللہ پر اعتقادہی نہیں رکھتے اس لئے یہ پڑھے بھی تو اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہے اس لئے شکار حلال نہیں ہو گا۔ آیت میں ہے۔ ولا تأكلوا مما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه وانه لفسق (ج) ( آیت ۲۱ ۱ سورۃ الانعام ۶) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ بسم اللہ نہ پڑھا ہو تو اس کو مت کھاؤ۔ اور شکار کی حدیث تو پہلے کئی بار گزر چکی ہے اس لئے ان لوگوں کا شکار حلال نہیں (۳) اثر میں ہے۔ عن جابر قــال نـهـي عـن ذبيحة المجوسى وصيــد کـلـبـه وطــائـره (و) (سنن للبیہقی ، باب ماجاء فی صید المجوسی ج تاسع ص ۱۱ نمبر ۱۸۹۲۶ر مصنف ابن ابی هیبته ۷ فی صید کلب المشرک والجھی والیہودی والنصرانی ج رابع ص ۲۳۲ نمبر ۱۹۶۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مجوی اور اس کے تحت میں مرتد اور بت پرست کا شکار حلال نہیں ہے۔
[2596] (17) And the hunt of a Magian, an apostate, and an idolater shall not be eaten. If a Magian, apostate, or idolater hunted with his dog or arrow, and the animal died before a Muslim slaughtered it, then that hunt is not halal. (1) These people are neither Muslims nor People of the Book, so just as their slaughter (dhabh) is not halal, their hunt is also not halal. (2) In the hadith it is mentioned that at the time of hunting, and in the verse it is mentioned that at the time of slaughter, one must recite Bismillah, then it will be halal. And these people do not believe in Bismillah, so even if they recite it, it is not considered. Therefore, their hunt is not halal. In the verse: 'And do not eat of that over which the name of Allah has not been mentioned, for indeed, it is grave disobedience.' (Quran 6:121). From this verse it is understood that if Bismillah is not recited, do not eat it. And the hadith about hunting has passed several times, so the hunt of these people is not halal. (3) In an athar: From Jabir, he said: 'He forbade the slaughter of the Magian and the hunt of his dog and his bird.' (w) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter on the Hunt of the Magian, Vol. 9, p. 11, no. 18926; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 7, on the hunt of the dog of the polytheist, the Magian, the Jew, and the Christian, Vol. 4, p. 232, no. 1969). From this athar it is understood that the hunt of the Magian and, under that, the apostate and idolater, is not halal.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Thamiri states a clear prohibition: the game killed by a Magian (fire-worshipper), an apostate (from Islam), or an idolater is not permissible to eat. The reasoning is threefold: first, these individuals are not Muslims and not People of the Book (Jews/Christians), so by analogy with their slaughtered meat, their game is also unlawful. Second, the act of hunting requires recitation of Bismillah based on Quran 6:121 and the hadith on hunting; since they do not believe in Allah, their recitation is invalid. Third, there is a specific tradition from Jabir forbidding the slaughter and hunt of the Magian. The rule thus extends to all who are not recognized as having a valid scripture-based religion.

Key Terms
مجوسی (majusi): A Magian, a follower of Zoroastrianism, considered neither Muslim nor People of the Book in the Hanafi school.
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate from Islam; his actions are invalid, including hunting.
وثنی (wathani): Idolater; one who worships idols, not a believer in the One God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hunt of a Magian not halal according to the Hanafi school?
A) Magians are not allowed to own dogs.
B) Magians do not recite Bismillah when hunting.
C) Magians are neither Muslims nor People of the Book, and their recitation of Bismillah is invalid.
D) Magians hunt at night only.

Key Takeaway
Game killed by a Magian, apostate, or idolater is haram because they lack valid belief and the required Bismillah.

Ownership of Game When First Shot Fails to Incapacitate
Determines ownership and permissibility when first shot fails to disable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of game when two hunters shoot and the first does not incapacitate the prey, and explain the evidence (dalil) from hadith.

Core Idea
Ownership of hunted game requires effective incapacitation, not merely hitting the target.

In this lesson, we examine a specific hunting scenario where two arrows strike the same animal, but only the second shot kills it. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling on who owns the prey and why, based on the concept of effective capture (ihraz) and supported by a general hadith. Understanding this ruling depends on grasping the criterion of incapacitation (nidhal) and how it applies to unowned property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State clearly which hunter owns the prey and whether it is permissible to eat.
2
Extract the Legal Criterion
Identify why the first hunter did not gain ownership: the prey was not incapacitated (nidhal) and remained in its means of escape (hayyiz al-imtina').
3
Analyze the Dalil
Examine the hadith cited: 'Whoever precedes to something that no Muslim has preceded to, it is his' (Abu Dawood). Note its original context and how it is applied by analogy (qiyas) to hunting.
The hadith is about dead/baren land, but the general wording supports the principle that unowned things become owned by whoever first effectively takes possession.
4
Confirm the Basis for Halal
The second hunter killed the prey with a proper shot (reciting Bismillah) while it was still wild, making it halal.
5
Summarize the Hanafi Position
Restate the rule: prey belongs to the one who incapacitates it such that capture is easy; merely hitting does not suffice.

۲۵۹۷] (۱۸) کسی نے شکار کو تیر مارا پس اس کو لگا لیکن اس کو نڈھال نہیں کیا نہ اس کو اپنے بچاؤ سے نکالا پھر دوسرے نے اس کو مارا اور اس کو قتل کر دیا تو شکار دوسرے کا ہوگا اور کھایا جائے گا۔ ایک آدمی نے شکار کو تیر مارا وہ لگا تو ضرور لیکن پھر بھی شکار بھاگتا رہا اور نڈھال نہیں ہوا جیسے صحتمند شکار بھاگتا ہے اس لئے یہ عام شکار کی طرح ہی ہے اور پہلے کا شکار نہیں ہوا۔ اب دوسرے آدمی نے تیر مار کر مار دیا تو شکار دوسرے کا ہوگا ۔ اور چونکہ دوسرے نے بسم اللہ پڑھ کر قتل کیا ہے اس لئے کھایا جائے گا وہ حلال ہے۔ پہلے کی مار سے شکار صحتمند شکار ہی رہا وہ پالتو کی طرح مجبور نہیں ہو گیا اس لئے وہ پہلے والے کا شکار نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ شکار اس کی ملکیت ہے جس نے مار کر نڈھال کر دیا کہ اب آسانی سے اس کو پکڑ سکتا ہے۔ اور دوسرے آدمی نے گویا کہ صحتمند شکار کو تیر سے قتل کیا اس لئے یہ شکار دوسرے آدمی کا ہے۔ اور شکار کی حالت میں مرگیا اس لئے حلال ہے (۲) دوسرے آدمی کے شکار ہونے کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابيها اسمر بن مضرس قال اتيت النبي الله فبايعته فقال من سبق الى ما لم يسبقه اليه مسلم فهو له (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی اقطاع الارضین ص ۷۸ نمبر ۳۰۷) یہ حدیث اگر چہ مردہ اور بنجر زمین کے بارے میں ہے لیکن عام جملہ سے استدلال کیا جا سکتا ہے کہ عام مسلمانوں کے لئے مباح چیز جو لے لے اس کی ہوگی۔ اس لئے شکار کو جو مار کر نڈھال کرے گا اسی کا ہوگا۔
(2597) (18) Someone shot an arrow at prey, and it hit but did not incapacitate it nor remove it from its means of escape. Then another person shot and killed it. So the prey belongs to the second person and will be eaten. A man shot an arrow at the prey; it hit but the prey continued running and did not become incapacitated, like healthy prey runs. So it is like general prey and did not become the first person's prey. Now the second person shot and killed it, so the prey belongs to the second person. And since the second person recited Bismillah and killed it, it will be eaten; it is halal. Because of the first person's shot, the prey remained healthy, it did not become constrained like a domestic animal, so it will not belong to the first person. For the prey belongs to the one who shot and incapacitated it such that he could easily capture it. And the second person effectively killed healthy prey with an arrow, so this prey is the second person's. And it died in the state of hunting, so it is halal. (2) The proof for the second person's ownership is this hadith: 'Whoever precedes to something that no Muslim has preceded to, it is his.' (Abu Dawood, no. 307) Although this hadith is about dead and barren land, it can be used as general evidence that anything permissible for common Muslims, whoever takes it first becomes its owner. So whoever shoots and incapacitates prey becomes its owner.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with its proof. The scenario: two hunters shoot at the same wild animal. The first arrow hits but does not incapacitate the animal; it remains free and able to flee. Then a second arrow kills it. The ruling: the prey belongs to the second hunter and is halal to eat. The author explains that since the first shot did not disable the animal, it did not enter his ownership. Ownership of hunted game is established when the hunter effectively captures it, i.e., incapacitates it so that escape is no longer possible. The second hunter's kill is valid because he recited Bismillah and the animal died from the shot. The hadith 'Whoever precedes to something that no Muslim has preceded to, it is his' is cited as proof, applied by analogy (qiyas) to wild game: just as unclaimed land becomes the property of whoever first develops it, unowned prey becomes the property of whoever first incapacitates it. The Hanafi school thus holds that ownership is based on effective capture (ihraz), not merely hitting.

Key Terms
نڈھال (nidhal): Incapacitated, unable to escape; the condition that establishes ownership of game.
حيز الامتناع (hayyiz al-imtina'): The state of being able to escape; the prey's means of self-defense or flight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two hunters shoot at a deer, the first arrow wounds it but it continues running, and the second arrow kills it, who owns the deer according to the Hanafi school?
A) The first hunter, because he hit it first
B) The second hunter, because the first did not incapacitate it
C) Both share equally
D) It remains ownerless

Key Takeaway
Ownership of hunted game requires effective incapacitation (nidhal); merely hitting the prey does not transfer ownership.

Incapacitation and Killing by Different Hunters
Determines ownership and permissibility when two hunters act on same prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership and permissibility when a first hunter incapacitates prey and a second kills it, and explain the proofs from hadith and athar.

Core Idea
Ownership to incapacitator, permissibility requires slaughter while alive.

The law of hunting must resolve a common scenario: one hunter wounds an animal so it cannot flee, and another finishes it. The Hanafi school teaches that ownership vests in the first hunter by virtue of effective capture (athkhana), but the animal is not halal unless slaughtered while alive. This ruling is rooted in prophetic instruction and the practice of early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The first hunter's arrow incapacitates the prey so it cannot escape. The second hunter then kills it with a projectile.
2
Determine ownership
Once the prey is incapacitated, it becomes the property of the first hunter. The second hunter's kill does not transfer ownership.
3
Determine permissibility
The prey is not halal to eat because it was found alive by the first hunter and should have been slaughtered (dhabh) rather than killed by a later projectile.
4
Examine the proofs
The hadith from Adi ibn Hatim (Muslim) commands slaughter if found alive. The athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i forbids eating if the animal dies in hand while still having ramaq (life).
5
Apply the ruling
If the first hunter had slaughtered the incapacitated animal, it would be halal. If the animal was dead when the first hunter reached it, different rules apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

پہلے آدمی نے شکار کو اتنا مارا کہ وہ نڈھال ہو گیا اور بھاگنے کے قابل نہیں رہا اس لئے یہ شکار حقیقت میں اسی کا ہو گیا۔ بعد میں دوسرے آدمی نے تیر مار کر ہلاک کیا تو اس کا نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ پہلے نے نڈھال کیا ہے اور شکار اسی کا ہو گیا ہے۔
حدیث اوپر گزرگئی۔
اور کھایا اس لئے نہیں جائے گا کہ وہ زندہ ہاتھ آ گیا تھا اس لئے اس کو ذبح کر کے کھانا چاہئے تھا لیکن ذبح حقیقی کرنے کی بجائے تیر سے ہلاک کر دیا اس لئے حلال نہیں ہے۔ حدیث گزر چکی ہے۔ عن عدی بن حاتم قال قال لي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اذا ارسلت كلبك فاذكر اسم الله فان امسك عليك فادركته حيا فاذبحه (ب) (مسلم شریف، باب الصيد بالكلاب المعلمۃ والرمي ص ۱۴۵ نمبر ۴۹۸۱/۱۹۲۹)(۲) اور اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابراهيم قال اذا اخذت الصيد و به رمق فمات في يدك فلا تأكله ( ج ) ( مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ۸ الرجل يأخذ الصيد و به رمق ج رابع ص ۲۴۳ نمبر ۱۹۲۲۵) اس حدیث اور اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ اس شکار میں رمق تھی اس لئے اس کو ذبح کرنا چاہئے تھا لیکن حاشیہ : (الف) میں حضور کے پاس آیا اور آپ سے بیعت کی ، آپ نے فرمایا جس چیز کی طرف کوئی مسلمان نہ بڑھا ہو کوئی اس کو بڑھ کر لے لے تو وہ اس کی ہے (ب) آپ نے مجھ سے فرمایا اگر اپنے کتے شکار پر چھوڑو اور بسم اللہ پڑھو۔ پس اگر وہ آپ کے لئے روکا اور اس کو زندہ پایا تو اس کو ذبح کرے (ج) حضرت ابراہیم نے فرمایا اگر شکار پکڑو اس حال میں کہ اس کی زندگی کی رمق ہو اور تمہارے ہاتھ میں مرجائے تو اس کو مت کھاؤ۔
(2598) (19) And if the first one incapacitated it, then the second shot and killed it, then it belongs to the first and is not to be eaten. If the second killed it, then the prey belongs to him and will be eaten. The first man shot the prey so intensely that it became incapacitated and unable to flee, so this prey effectively became his. Afterwards, when the second man shot and killed it, it will not be his, because the first incapacitated it and it had become his. The hadith has passed above. And it will not be eaten because it came into his possession alive, so it should have been slaughtered. But instead of actual slaughter, he killed it with an arrow, so it is not halal. The hadith has already passed: from Adi ibn Hatim: the Messenger of Allah said, 'If you send your dog and mention the name of Allah, and it catches for you, and you find it alive, then slaughter it.' (Muslim, no. 1929) (2) And there is an athar from Ibrahim: 'If you catch the prey while it still has life (ramaq) and it dies in your hand, do not eat it.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 19225) From this hadith and athar it is understood that this prey had life (ramaq), so it should have been slaughtered, but instead it was killed by an arrow, so it is not halal. Footnote: (a) 'I came to the Prophet and pledged allegiance, and he said: Whoever precedes to something that no Muslim has preceded to, it is his.' (b) 'If you send your dog and mention the name of Allah, and it catches for you and you find it alive, then slaughter it.' (c) 'If you catch the prey while it still has life and it dies in your hand, do not eat it.'
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and its proofs. The scenario: the first hunter's arrow incapacitates the prey so it cannot escape; then the second hunter shoots and kills it. The ruling: the prey belongs to the first hunter, but it is not halal to eat. The reason: once incapacitated, the prey becomes the property of the first hunter (by virtue of effective capture). The second hunter's act of killing it does not transfer ownership. However, the prey is not halal because when the first hunter approached it alive, he should have slaughtered it properly (dhabh) to make it halal. Instead, it was killed by a projectile without the required slaughter. The hadith from Adi ibn Hatim and the athar from Ibrahim are cited to support that if an animal is found alive, it must be slaughtered; otherwise, it is carrion (murdar). This is a key distinction: if the first hunter had immediately slaughtered it, it would be halal; but by letting it be killed by a later shot, it becomes impermissible. The Hanafi ruling emphasizes that the method of killing must be proper for the state of the animal.

Key Terms
اثخن (athkhana): To incapacitate, to render unable to escape; the first stage of ownership.
رمق (ramaq): The last vestige of life; the state between life and death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a first hunter incapacitates a deer and a second hunter kills it with an arrow, is the meat halal?
A) Yes, because the second hunter recited Bismillah
B) Yes, because it belongs to the first hunter
C) No, because it was not slaughtered while alive
D) No, because it belongs to no one

Key Takeaway
If prey is incapacitated (athkhana) and later killed without slaughter, it is owned by the incapacitator but is carrion.

Liability of Second Hunter for Value of Game
Ruling on second hunter's liability for game's value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation owed by a second hunter who kills a game already owned by a first hunter, applying the Hanafi rule that liability is limited to the value after the first's injury.

Core Idea
A tortfeasor is liable only for the actual loss he caused — the first hunter's own injury reduces the compensable value.

When a game becomes the property of a hunter through incapacitation, any subsequent interference is a trespass against that ownership. This passage establishes the precise compensation when a second hunter kills that game: he must pay its value at the moment of his act, not the original healthy value. The first hunter's own wounding reduces the value and therefore reduces the second's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
State the ruling: the second hunter is liable (damin) for the value of the game to the first. Note that the game had already become the first hunter's property by incapacitation.
Ownership by incapacitation is a key Hanafi principle in hunting law.
2
Recognize the limitation on liability
The second hunter's liability is reduced by the decrease in value caused by the first hunter's wound. This is stated as 'excluding what his wound decreased in value.'
3
Understand the rationale (talil)
The reason: the first hunter himself caused part of the loss. The second hunter is liable only for the actual damage he caused, not for pre-existing damage. This applies the general principle that compensation equals the loss inflicted.
The text explicitly says: 'Because the first man himself reduced the value by wounding it.'
4
Apply the example
Walk through the numeric example: healthy value 10 dirhams, after wound 4 dirhams. Second hunter pays 4 dirhams.
If the first hunter had not wounded, the second would pay 10 dirhams.
5
Formulate the underlying principles
Extract the two usul: (1) the game becomes the first's property upon incapacitation, (2) a tortfeasor compensates only for the loss he directly causes.
These principles apply broadly in Hanafi tort law (ghasb and itlaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني ضامن لقيمته للاول غير ما نقصته جراحته [ ۲۶۰۰] (۲۱) ويجوز اصطياد ما يوكل لحمه من الحيوان وما لايوكل.
قتل کر دیا اس لئے وہ حلال نہیں رہا۔
[۲۵۹۹] (۲۰) اور دوسرا آدمی پہلے کے لئے قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا اس کے علاوہ جو نقصان کیا اس کے زخمی کرنے نے ۔
ا شکار مکمل طور پر پہلے آدمی کا ہو چکا ہے اس لئے دوسرے آدمی نے اس شکار کو ہلاک کیا تو اس پر پہلے آدمی کے لئے شکار کا تاوان لازم ہوگا۔ البتہ شکار کو پہلے آدمی نے زخمی کیا تو دیکھا جائے گا کہ زخمی کرنے کے بعد شکار کی قیمت کیا رہ گئی وہ قیمت لازم ہوگی ۔ مثلا وہ جانور صحیح سالم ہوتا تو اس کی قیمت دس درہم تھی اور زخمی ہونے کے بعد اس کی قیمت چار درہم رہ گئی تو دوسرے آدمی پر چار درہم ہی لازم ہوں گے۔ کیونکہ پہلے آدمی نے خود زخمی کر کے شکار کی قیمت کم کر دی ہے۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ شکار پہلے آدمی کا ہو چکا ہے۔ اور دوسرا اصول یہ ہے کہ دوسرے نے جتنا ضائع کیا ہے اتنا ہی تاوان لازم ہوگا زیادہ نہیں۔
And the second is liable for its value to the first, excluding what his wound decreased in value. [2600] (21) And it is permissible to hunt animals whose meat is eaten and those whose meat is not eaten.
Because he killed it, so it is not halal.
[2599] (20) And the second man will be liable for the value to the first, excluding the damage caused by his wounding. This game had completely become the property of the first man, therefore when the second man killed it, he becomes liable for compensation to the first. However, the first man had wounded the game, so it will be seen what the value of the game was after the wound; that value will be obligatory. For example, if the animal was healthy, its value was ten dirhams, and after being wounded its value became four dirhams, then the second man is liable for only four dirhams. Because the first man himself reduced the value by wounding it. The principle: this issue is based on the principle that the game had become the property of the first. And the second principle is that the second man is liable only for what he destroyed, not more.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage states the legal consequence when the second hunter kills a game that was already incapacitated and owned by the first hunter. The second hunter is liable to pay compensation (daman) to the first hunter for the value of the game. However, the amount is calculated based on the value of the game after the first hunter's wound, not the full healthy value. The rationale is twofold: first, the game had already become the property of the first hunter through incapacitation; second, liability is only for the actual damage caused. The first hunter's injury reduced the value, so the second hunter compensates only for the remaining value (the part he destroyed). The example clarifies: if the healthy game was worth ten dirhams, but after the first's wound it was only worth four dirhams, the second must pay four dirhams. The underlying legal principles (usul) are: (1) ownership vests in the first hunter upon incapacitation, and (2) the tortfeasor is liable only for the actual loss caused, no more.

Key Terms
ضامن (damin): Legally liable to pay compensation for destroying another's property.
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value of the game at the time of destruction, taking into account any prior damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, how is the amount of compensation determined that the second hunter must pay to the first?
A) The full market value of the game when healthy.
B) The value of the game after the first hunter's wound.
C) The cost of the second hunter's arrow.
D) No compensation is required because the game was already owned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a tortfeasor's liability is limited to the actual loss he causes; prior damage by the owner reduces the compensable value.

Permissibility of Hunting All Animals
Ruling on the permissibility of hunting both edible and non-edible animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting non-edible animals and explain the Quranic proof used to derive it.

Core Idea
Hunting is permissible for all animals, edible or not, based on the general permission in the Quran after leaving ihram.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from the Hanafi matn: it is permissible to hunt both edible and non-edible animals. The author supports this with a Quranic verse and a practical rationale. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi position on the permissibility of utilizing animals beyond mere consumption.

Guided Path
1
State the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Hunting (istiyad) of animals. Ruling: permissible (mubah) for both edible and non-edible.
2
Identify the Quranic proof
The verse from Surah al-Ma'idah 5:2 (part): 'wa idha halaltum fas'tadu' (when you are free from ihram, then hunt). This is the dalil.
3
Analyze the linguistic indication
The imperative form 'fas'tadu' indicates permissibility according to Hanafi usul (al-amr li al-ibahah). The word 'sayd' is general, covering all animals.
4
Consider the opposing view
Some schools restrict hunting to edible animals only; the Hanafi position includes non-edible ones, supported by general permission and additional purpose (skin, hair, bones).
5
Confirm the Hanafi position
The mufta bihi is the permissibility for all, as stated in the matn.

Source Text

[۲۶۰۰] (۳۱) ويجوز اصطياد ما يوكل لحمه من الحيوان وما لايوكل. [2600] (31) It is permissible to hunt animals whose meat is eaten and those whose meat is not eaten. [2600] (31) And it is permissible to hunt animals whose meat is eaten and those whose meat is not eaten. You may hunt both those animals whose meat is eaten and those whose meat is not eaten. In the verse it says: 'And when you are free from Ihram, then hunt' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:2, part of verse). This verse gives a general command for hunting, whether the animal is one whose meat is eaten or not. Therefore, hunting both is halal. (2) Hunting is not only for eating meat, but also for skin, hair, and bones, so hunting non-edible animals is also halal; there is no prohibition. 'Istada' is derived from 'sayd'; here it comes in the form of 'ifti'al' meaning to hunt. Footnote: (a) 'When you become halal, then you may hunt.'

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn states that it is permissible to hunt both edible and non-edible animals. The Urdu commentary explains that the Quranic verse (5:2) gives a general permission for hunting after leaving ihram, which includes all types of animals. Additionally, hunting is not only for meat but also for other uses like skin, hair, and bones, so there is no restriction. The footnote quotes the relevant part of the verse. The commentary also notes the linguistic derivation of 'istada' from 'sayd'.

Key Terms

ما يوكل لحمه (ma yu'kalu lahmuhu): Animal whose meat is eaten (edible).
ما لا يوكل (ma la yu'kalu): Animal whose meat is not eaten (non-edible).

Conditions for Halal Slaughter by Kitabi
Ruling with proof on slaughter conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for reciting Bismillah in slaughter by a Kitabi and apply the ruling to evaluate the status of European Christians.

Core Idea
Slaughter is halal only if Bismillah is recited—whether by Muslim or Kitabi.

The Hanafi school permits the slaughtered meat of Muslims and People of the Book (Jews and Christians). However, this permission is not unconditional—the slaughterer must recite Bismillah. This lesson explores the Quranic proof from Surah al-Ma'idah and the interpretation of Ibn Abbas, and then addresses a critical modern question: can we eat meat from European Christians who do not recite Bismillah?

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states: 'The slaughter of a Muslim and a Kitabi is halal.' Identify the act (dhabihah) and the initial hukm (halal).
This ruling is subject to conditions derived from the proofs.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The proof is Quran 5:5: 'اليوم أحل لكم الطيبات وطعام الذين أوتوا الكتاب حل لكم'. Identify the verse and its apparent meaning.
The verse mentions 'ta'am' (food) which is then interpreted.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Ibn Abbas (RA) interprets 'ta'am' as 'dhabaih' (slaughtered animals). This establishes that the verse permits the meat of Ahl al-Kitab.
The Hanafi school relies on this tafsir to include their slaughter.
4
Identify the condition added
The commentary cites a narration from al-Zuhri: 'لا بأس بذبيحة نصارى العرب، وإن سمعته يسمى لغير الله فلا تأكل'. This implies that if he invokes other than Allah, do not eat. The condition of reciting Bismillah is derived.
Thus, the slaughter is halal only if Bismillah is recited; if he invokes other than Allah or omits Bismillah, it is not halal.
5
Apply to modern scenario
European Christians are described as heretics who do not recite Bismillah. Therefore, their slaughter is not halal. Also, Umar (RA) considered Arab Christians of his time not Ahl al-Kitab, reinforcing caution.
The practical ruling: meat from European Christian sources is not halal unless one is certain that Bismillah was recited.
